  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_4ca9bd45-e549-4d5b-bb60-7c8813a695d1'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13637021067758177352">El Ramayana es un libro de la India antigua. Se cree que sus orígenes se remontan al siglo IV a.C. ya que forma parte de los smíriti hindúes (textos no aportados directamente por Dios, sino transmitidos por la tradición). El Ramayana es una de las obras literarias más importantes de la India, este libro estableció algunas pautas fundamentales dentro de la poesía sánscrita </span>
  <span id="freeText13637021067758177352" style="display:none">El Ramayana es un libro de la India antigua. Se cree que sus orígenes se remontan al siglo IV a.C. ya que forma parte de los smíriti hindúes (textos no aportados directamente por Dios, sino transmitidos por la tradición). El Ramayana es una de las obras literarias más importantes de la India, este libro estableció algunas pautas fundamentales dentro de la poesía sánscrita y ha tenido una gran influencia en el arte y la cultura del sureste de Asia, El texto de esta obra es alegórico definitivamente, cuenta la historia del semidiós Rama, quien representa todo lo bueno, y su encantada lucha contra los asuras, que ostentan el poder del mal. En la mitología hindú, asura es el símbolo equivalente al titán o demonio gigante, así que en esta epopeya tenemos el mito de la lucha entre semidioses y demonios, es decir, el combate entre los poderes de la luz y las fuerzas malignas de la oscuridad.<br /><br />Rama constituye el ideal del hombre sabio, fuerte, justo y bueno, en él se funden todas las cualidades del gobernante y del guerrero. Además, tiene magníficos recursos, que utiliza en el momento oportuno.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13637021067758177352" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_781696316" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/781696316" />
    <a title="Daren" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11995655-daren"><img alt="Daren" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1347678806p2/11995655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/781696316?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daren" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daren" href="/user/show/11995655-daren">Daren</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788120817203" href="/book/show/960846.Ramayana">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=indian-author">indian-author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=indian-subcontinent">indian-subcontinent</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11995655-daren?shelf=5-star">5-star</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer781696316" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2410075796142989542">This was a great read. This is a re-telling of the Ramayana, by William Buck. It is not in verse, although it remains in the original seven parts, and is considered one of the better modern re-tellings.<br/>I must admit, the first of the seven parts almost had me putting this back on the self for another time. It was confusing with the number of characters, the timeframes and general confusion. I am so glad I persisted, as from the end of that part on, it was brilliant. To be fair it was the scene se</span>
  <span id="freeText2410075796142989542" style="display:none">This was a great read. This is a re-telling of the Ramayana, by William Buck. It is not in verse, although it remains in the original seven parts, and is considered one of the better modern re-tellings.<br />I must admit, the first of the seven parts almost had me putting this back on the self for another time. It was confusing with the number of characters, the timeframes and general confusion. I am so glad I persisted, as from the end of that part on, it was brilliant. To be fair it was the scene setting section of the book and was trying to set out the history. I should probably go back and re-read it, because I really don't know what it was...<br /><br />For me, from that point on, the characters were absorbing, the story paced well and interesting and there were stories inside the story which explained aspects of the narrative. The other aspect of this edition of the book are the pictures. They occur probably every 20-30 pages, and look like pencil sketches. No colour, limited contrast - by which I mean they are not boldly drawn, but subtly drawn, and they are excellent. To me they seem to capture the intent of the writing, and provide realistic Indian imagery of the characters - not just people, but the mythological creatures and the animals. It does have a high proportion of inevitably beautiful women who appear almost exclusively with minimal clothing, usually topless, which was probably slightly disconnected from the text.<br /><br />So no plot lines in the review - they would be reduced to something too simple, or would remain over complex, and there are plenty of sources or reviews which can assist. The story is somewhat familiar to me, but having not been brought up with the story as a part of my culture, there are plenty or moral lessons to take from this story, and it is plain to see why it remains popular and important in Indian culture.<br /><br />So for stars, I struggled with the opening section, but loved the rest. I was settling for 4 stars, but I liked it better than that in the end, so racks up my first five star rating of 2016.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2410075796142989542" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1331617990" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1331617990" />

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1331617990?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fatin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fatin" href="/user/show/12904385-fatin">Fatin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/6470578-ramayana">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12904385-fatin?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12904385-fatin?shelf=epics">epics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12904385-fatin?shelf=from-the-subcontinent">from-the-subcontinent</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1331617990" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7884215018081217637">I love readings Epics and old, old mythologies and really making the connections between them all over the world. Icarus burning his wings in Greek mythology to Sampati, the vulture, who burned his wings on account of protecting his brother when they both flew too close to the sun in Ramayana. There are some obvious parallels like this one, or another one between Hindu religion and the Abrahamic faiths that I discovered when talking to a friend. Krishna is carried across a river in a basket when</span>
  <span id="freeText7884215018081217637" style="display:none">I love readings Epics and old, old mythologies and really making the connections between them all over the world. Icarus burning his wings in Greek mythology to Sampati, the vulture, who burned his wings on account of protecting his brother when they both flew too close to the sun in Ramayana. There are some obvious parallels like this one, or another one between Hindu religion and the Abrahamic faiths that I discovered when talking to a friend. Krishna is carried across a river in a basket when a king starts killing male babies because he hears of a prophecy that one of them will rise to kill him which is so similar to the story of Moses I've heard. The Hindu Gods also resemble the Greek Gods; Indra - Zeus, Vayu - Eurus. But what I really love about Epics is that you can sort of trace back all fantasy fiction to it. You can also see the trend of black and white truths and, of course, the rampant sexism. The part that's always bothered me the most. This one goes on to feminizing the land and calling the King the husband of the land and with his death comes the widowhood of the land. <br /><br />Random musings:<br />- All the old myths and epics I've read always describe the Men as beautiful, having slender faces and almond-shaped eyes and smooth complexion. All attributes that are now seen as feminine.<br />- Ew at the concept of a woman existing to serve her husband. and the idea of a man having several wives and consorts. <br />- Also had the plotline of a man going near insane with grief on losing his lover.<br />- I haven't read book 7 of this Epic. I read it in my Norton Anthology of world literature so parts of the previous books + book 7 are missing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7884215018081217637" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1219973920" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5851914351320942300">This classic translation of the Ramayana is a complete and unabridged verse by verse translation of the great epic poem. This is not the Ramayana in the story format but the translation of the verses in the poetic form. The author reproduces the spirit of the ancient hymns with great flair. The lavishly added notes to the verses and appendix at the end of the book adds to the reference value of the title.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1500664121" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

Reading this book would be part of classic education in India but of course not part of Western. Western education gives books that just keep reinforcing each other. That is why everything seems like "common sense" to those who have not wandered out of their culture of birth.<br /><br />Take a chance -- look at a different world.</span>

<div id="review_35807581" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/35807581" />
    <a title="Josh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1640172-josh"><img alt="Josh" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/35807581?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh" href="/user/show/1640172-josh">Josh</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer35807581" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9631883949334232049">Some translations value accuracy and fidelity to the original text above all else. They work hard to preserve the meter and rhyme scheme (if any) and stay as close as possible to a word-for-word imitation, even if the result sounds a bit clumsy. I suspect that this book is not one of those translations. Buck is clearly a master storyteller in his own right, and his prose flows so well in English that it's hard to imagine he isn't taking some liberties. I would recommend this book to anyone who w</span>
  <span id="freeText9631883949334232049" style="display:none">Some translations value accuracy and fidelity to the original text above all else. They work hard to preserve the meter and rhyme scheme (if any) and stay as close as possible to a word-for-word imitation, even if the result sounds a bit clumsy. I suspect that this book is not one of those translations. Buck is clearly a master storyteller in his own right, and his prose flows so well in English that it's hard to imagine he isn't taking some liberties. I would recommend this book to anyone who wants to read a good story. Others will have to say how accurate it is.</span>

<div id="review_610145106" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/610145106" />
    <a title="carlageek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17679151-carlageek"><img alt="carlageek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361456132p2/17679151.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/610145106?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carlageek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="carlageek" href="/user/show/17679151-carlageek">carlageek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer610145106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16135800975403975820">This is the only adaptation of the Ramayana I have read, and so I can't be sure how much of my poor rating is attributable to Buck's adaptation and how much is dissatisfaction with the epic itself. So, I will list the aspects I find lacking, and those better versed than I in the mythology can interpret accordingly.<br/><br/>Although there are a few memorable lines ("Raavana lay like a collection of wrongs") for the most part I find the language cloying, dumbed-down amateur poetry using hackneyed, not terr</span>
  <span id="freeText16135800975403975820" style="display:none">This is the only adaptation of the Ramayana I have read, and so I can't be sure how much of my poor rating is attributable to Buck's adaptation and how much is dissatisfaction with the epic itself. So, I will list the aspects I find lacking, and those better versed than I in the mythology can interpret accordingly.<br /><br />Although there are a few memorable lines ("Raavana lay like a collection of wrongs") for the most part I find the language cloying, dumbed-down amateur poetry using hackneyed, not terribly evocative imagery. The story itself is a drag - like an interminable action film, battle scene after battle scene that just aren't that interesting. I had expected to learn something about Laxman and his relationship with Ram, but very little of this is explored. Likewise Sita - we are told over and over again that she is the most beautiful woman in the world but really, who cares? She does not develop any kind of personality until she butts heads with Raavana. As for Raavana, I understand he is supposed to be preternaturally smart, having learned the entirety of the Vedas in one day. But Buck's Raavana is, in short, a moron. <br /><br />Finally, what happened to Sita's trial by fire? I was stunned to find that episode missing. Is it not present in all versions of the epic?<br /><br />One has the feeling of a mythology dumbed down and cleansed - the good guys are all physically beautiful and morally pure, the bad guys are all ugly and stupid. Yawn. Is the Ramayana really so simplistic? Where is all the nuance and ambiguity of the Mahabharata? If you just want to dip into Hindu mythology for the first time, Devdutt Pattnaik's Mahabharata is a much more satisfying place to start. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16135800975403975820" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_281730174" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/281730174" />
    <a title="Gillian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4890171-gillian"><img alt="Gillian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334890819p2/4890171.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/281730174?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gillian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gillian" href="/user/show/4890171-gillian">Gillian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788172763657" href="/book/show/141155.Ramayana">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer281730174" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3685491343275131070">The reason I'm giving this five stars is because the author provides a detailed glossary of anglicized sanskrit words, as well as a guide to pronouncing sanskrit vowels. While he chooses not to include a translation of what he calls "the legend" of Sita's betrayal, he still provides an extremely detailed translation of Valmiki's text as it is, including some translation from the poet Kamban's version of the Ramayana (who translated it into Tamil). However, this book is very simply put forward, a</span>
  <span id="freeText3685491343275131070" style="display:none">The reason I'm giving this five stars is because the author provides a detailed glossary of anglicized sanskrit words, as well as a guide to pronouncing sanskrit vowels. While he chooses not to include a translation of what he calls "the legend" of Sita's betrayal, he still provides an extremely detailed translation of Valmiki's text as it is, including some translation from the poet Kamban's version of the Ramayana (who translated it into Tamil). However, this book is very simply put forward, as he says, for children and mothers. The sentences are simple and short, while not boring to more advanced adult readers. He presents the text like a teacher, stopping the narrative occasionally to remind us of something, or to discuss a character's moral action or a writing tool used by Valmiki or Kamban. The author's own Gandhian belief system is apparent, so if you're looking for an unbiased translation don't look here. But if you're looking for a simple yet beautiful translation with context, this is the version you want. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3685491343275131070" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

These pieces make the reading very interesting as it helps you appreciate different views they took which very well could be a reflection of their time, socio-cultural change.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_292421135" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/292421135" />
    <a title="woodshadows" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5420338-woodshadows"><img alt="woodshadows" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363551809p2/5420338.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/292421135?book_show_action=true">Mar 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="woodshadows" class="user" itemprop="url" name="woodshadows" href="/user/show/5420338-woodshadows">woodshadows</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781409919612" href="/book/show/6409396-the-ramayan-of-valmiki---volume-i">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer292421135" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17301624215344968977">Oh ya.. I had written a nice long review and then I clicked somewhere on my screen and the entire review disintegrated before my very eyes. I meant to get back to it so let's see here..<br/><br/>First off, the translation I was using omitted several passages due to "containing sentiments not popular or proper in our society", I think he means some people had sex, or maybe they worked on sunday or ate bread that was leavened, who knows! obviously something seemed to offend this particular translators ideas</span>
  <span id="freeText17301624215344968977" style="display:none">Oh ya.. I had written a nice long review and then I clicked somewhere on my screen and the entire review disintegrated before my very eyes. I meant to get back to it so let's see here..<br /><br />First off, the translation I was using omitted several passages due to "containing sentiments not popular or proper in our society", I think he means some people had sex, or maybe they worked on sunday or ate bread that was leavened, who knows! obviously something seemed to offend this particular translators ideas of propriety. At other times he simply omitted entire chapters because "they were boring and repetitive", I'm not sure whether he deserves praise for hijacking this decision from me, but in any case considering I have no ability to omit reading even the most boring chapters or books I will just move along.<br /><br />This tale starts out very nice, but there were many things I really found distasteful, which I find in so much of hindu writing, maybe it's my own particular societal prejudices coming out, ironically though I profess to be a hindu of sorts I still find myself cringing at some of the ideas which hindus hold in highest esteem. This is probably going to contain all sorts of spoilers, fortunately if you have decided to read this behemoth of a story you probably have already read the summary, so choose as you like, to continue reading or not. This is what I don't like - Rama, who for no other reason than being "Rama" is treated like a God, funny considering he is considered an incarnation of God, nevertheless he never really does anything particularly special, yet everyone around him, his brothers, women, foreign kings etc, all dote upon him and his constantly butthurt feelings. The book constantly tells me that he is noble, honourable, brave, etc etc, but these traits are never shown in action, in fact much like Achilles he seems like more of a sulky little baby through much of the book. What really cemented my dislike for Rama was in the final pages. Now, some quick background here: Rama married Sita, Sita was kidnapped by Ravana and taken to his island refuge. Rama spends the book fighting demons and trying to win her back. Sweet romantic Arthurian type stuff here, right? Wrong. While a captive with Ravana, Ravana constantly tries to trick Sita into marrying him, telling her Rama is dead, offering her jewels, etc. Sita forebears every inducement and stays true to Rama, even at the risk of her own safety and comfort. Noble lady, Rama is a lucky guy, right? Again, wrong. Rama eventually vanquishes Ravana, or really, mostly it is through the efforts of his family and friends that he overcomes, through their deaths and sacrifices, they who will do everything blindly for Rama, simply because "Rama is Rama". So he gets Sita back. She comes up to him, ready to throw herself in his arms. We, the western readers are here expecting a happy reunion, the fruition of all the books struggles. But this is India, or specifically Hinduism, with it's sometimes warped sense of human relations. Rama scorns Sita, he basically spits in her face and laughs at her in disgust, he tells her that he didn't fight Ravana for HER (how presumptuous of the silly girl to think so!), he fought Ravana for his HONOUR (here the reader is supposed to glow with moral triumph - if said reader happens to have a warped sense of human relations). He rejects her and moves along, leaving a crushed Sita, wallowing in pain for Rama, who let's not forget she loves devotedly simply because he is Rama. He does this because he considers Sita "spoiled" for having been in the possession of another man (demon) and to associate with her would be a stain upon him. Sita decides well if I can't have my Rama I might as well self-immolate myself. This she does, "as any good Hindu wife should". Everyone gathers around to show Rama what happened and Sita is reborn and they live happily ever after with Sita fully acknowledging what an insignificant human she is and what a great and glorious man (God) Rama is - it's every Hindu man's greatest story to share with their wives and daughters, training generations of women into blind submission to their men. Vomit on this.. I'm not a rabid feminist but I have a good eye for propaganda designed to oppress. Next week we can read the Bhagavad Gita, in which Rama, now in the form of Krishna, counsels Arjuna to dispell his indecision about whether or not he should take part in a war against his cousins to slaughter them, Krishna counsels that rather he should blindly perform the slaughter of his cousins and do it in "God's name" as a "tool of God" and have his conscience clean.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17301624215344968977" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_226532156" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/226532156" />
    <a title="Sooraj Subramaniam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5985135-sooraj-subramaniam"><img alt="Sooraj Subramaniam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312763673p2/5985135.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/226532156?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sooraj Subramaniam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sooraj Subramaniam" href="/user/show/5985135-sooraj-subramaniam">Sooraj Subramaniam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer226532156" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer933565463734076322">It is no doubt a human tale, but there is some sentiment it evokes that I cannot put into words. Some tug just behind the tear-producing glands ...<br/><br/>I found that this telling captured how I'd felt about the Ramayana, having grown up listening to all the stories as part of nightcaps or preludes to evening prayers, living both unashamedly indian and unconfusedly western lives. It was honest and plain, glorious and abundant, rich and telling, and yet so bizarrely mysterious.<br/><br/>I remember the first time </span>
  <span id="freeText933565463734076322" style="display:none">It is no doubt a human tale, but there is some sentiment it evokes that I cannot put into words. Some tug just behind the tear-producing glands ...<br /><br />I found that this telling captured how I'd felt about the Ramayana, having grown up listening to all the stories as part of nightcaps or preludes to evening prayers, living both unashamedly indian and unconfusedly western lives. It was honest and plain, glorious and abundant, rich and telling, and yet so bizarrely mysterious.<br /><br />I remember the first time I read it and gaped in awe at Ravana's moment of curtain - certainly the most appealing twist I'd ever come across in this story. Indeed, the very essence of Hindu philosophy was found scribbled in one letter, sealed in stone tablet, from Ravana to Rama. Most clever!<br /><br />Yes, there were moments when I found the narrative difficult to follow: stumbling turns of phrases and awkward shifts between points of person. I don't know if this was because Buck wasn't a professional writer, or because a translation from ancient Sanskrit to modern English wouldn't simply lose much of the original sense. How will we ever know? Still, there is an essence of old, an unknown-ness somehow comforting, that this version captures and delivers. <br /><br />Those unacquainted with the premise of the Ramayana must remember that this is an epic; one of the most voluminous stories ever told. Imagine, it has shaped the course of civilisations over millenia! So they cannot get in expecting a light read over tea and cake. <br /><br />Verily, the hero must be Hanuman. For there seems no greater adventurer than he, living like a fuel caught ablaze in the momentary spark that is life. Jai!</span>
  <a data-text-id="933565463734076322" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_149377927" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/149377927" />
    <a title="PTS Books Club" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5002535-pts-books-club"><img alt="PTS Books Club" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1298088913p2/5002535.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/149377927?book_show_action=true">Feb 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="PTS Books Club" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PTS Books Club" href="/user/show/5002535-pts-books-club">PTS Books Club</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/322342.The_Ramayana">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer149377927" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11811778583500501812">A brilliant translation of the timeless epic, the Ramayana pervades the country’s moral and cultural consciousness. In her magnificent translation Arshia Sattar has successfully bridged both time and space to make this monumental ancient classic accessible to the present-day reader. <br/><br/>[Arshia Sattar (born 1960), an Indian translator, facilitator, author, and director, obtained her PhD in South Asian Languages and Civilizations from the University of Chicago in 1990.She has also worked with documen</span>
  <span id="freeText11811778583500501812" style="display:none">A brilliant translation of the timeless epic, the Ramayana pervades the country’s moral and cultural consciousness. In her magnificent translation Arshia Sattar has successfully bridged both time and space to make this monumental ancient classic accessible to the present-day reader. <br /><br />[Arshia Sattar (born 1960), an Indian translator, facilitator, author, and director, obtained her PhD in South Asian Languages and Civilizations from the University of Chicago in 1990.She has also worked with documentary film and theatre. Most recently, she taught Indian Studies at the Mahindra United World College of India in Pune for five years. She currently works as a freelance writer and researcher. She is also the programming director at OpenSpace, an NGO committed to promoting awareness of issues such as globalization.]<br /><br />Valmiki (ca. 400 BC?) is celebrated as the poet harbinger in Sanskrit literature. He is the author of the epic Ramayana, based on the attribution in the text of the epic itself. He is revered as the Adi Kavi, which means First Poet, for he discovered the first śloka i.e. first verse, which set the base and defined the form to Sanskrit poetry. The Yoga Vasistha is attributed to him. A religious movement called Valmikism is based on Valmiki's teachings as presented in the Ramayana and the Yoga Vasistha. At least by the 1st century AD, Valmiki's reputation as the father of Sanskrit classical poetry seems to have been legendary.<br /><br />The Rāmāyaṇa, originally written by Valmiki, consists of 24,000 verses in seven cantos (some say six i.e. excluding the Uttara Ramayana) (kāṇḍas). The Ramayana tells the story of a prince, Rama of Ayodhya, whose wife Sita is abducted by the demon (Rākshasa) king of Lanka, Rāvana. The Valmiki Ramayana is dated variously from 500 BC to 100 BC, or about co-eval with early versions of the Mahabhārata. As with many traditional epics, it has gone through a long process of interpolations and redactions, making it impossible to date accurately.<br /><br />One of the most important literary works of ancient India, the Ramayana has had a profound impact on art and culture in the Indian subcontinent and Southeast Asia. The story ushered in the tradition of the next thousand years of massive-scale works in the rich diction of regal courts and Brahminical temples. It has also inspired much secondary literature in various languages, notably the Kambaramayanam by the Tamil poet Kambar of the 13th century, the Telugu-language Molla Ramayana, 14th century Kannada poet Narahari's Torave Ramayan, and 15th century Bengali poet Krittibas Ojha's Krittivasi Ramayan, as well as the 16th century Awadhi version, Ramacharitamanas, written by Tulsidas.<br /><br />As in many oral epics, multiple versions of the Ramayana survive. In particular, the Ramayana related in North India differs in important respects from that preserved in South India and the rest of South-East Asia. There is an extensive tradition of oral storytelling based on the Ramayana in Indonesia, Cambodia, Thailand, Malaysia, Laos, Vietnam, and Maldives. Father Kamil Bulke, author of Ramakatha, has identified over 300 variants of Ramayana.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11811778583500501812" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2015707067" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2015707067" />
    <a title="Udit Srivastava" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/21757106-udit-srivastava"><img alt="Udit Srivastava" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1537115996p2/21757106.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2015707067?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Udit Srivastava" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Udit Srivastava" href="/user/show/21757106-udit-srivastava">Udit Srivastava</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788172763657" href="/book/show/141155.Ramayana">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2015707067" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16288950702418221865">There was a line in the final credits background song in Ramananda Sagar's Ramayana, which I fondly watched as a child- "Puni Puni Kitne hi Kahe Sunave, Jiya ki Pyaas bujhat na Bujhae", which loosely translates to-" Even after repeated re-tellings of this beautiful story of Shree Ram, the heart does not get tired of listening to it".<br/>This line broadly sums up my review for this book, which I wanted to write in detail. It is a long read and took me a while to complete. As most of the Indians, I wa</span>
  <span id="freeText16288950702418221865" style="display:none">There was a line in the final credits background song in Ramananda Sagar's Ramayana, which I fondly watched as a child- "Puni Puni Kitne hi Kahe Sunave, Jiya ki Pyaas bujhat na Bujhae", which loosely translates to-" Even after repeated re-tellings of this beautiful story of Shree Ram, the heart does not get tired of listening to it".<br />This line broadly sums up my review for this book, which I wanted to write in detail. It is a long read and took me a while to complete. As most of the Indians, I was aware of most of the events and narratives that build up this epic tale but novelty is not what I sought when I decided to take up this as my next read. <br />The excellent style of C. Rajagopalachari in presenting this story is the pudding on the cake. The book takes direct cues from Valmiki Ramayana, which focused more on the human side of Rama than the divine side, which is the focus in the versions of Tulasidas and Kamban. It takes you on the emotional journey and connects you to the human sides of the characters like Kaikeyi, Dasaratha, Bharata, Sita, Hanuman &amp; Kumbhakarana. The flow of the book is bit on the slower side, which helps the reader to get absorbed and empathize with the emotions that the characters are going through at a point of time.One of the most elaborate sections that deserve a special mention are- Dasaratha's longing for Rama at the time of his going on exile, Rama's pain after the abdustion of Sita and Kumbhakarana's dialogue with Ravana before the ultimate battle which addresses the moral dilemma that he faces.<br />One of the points that this book touches upon is the comparison of Vibhishana and Kumbhakarana and their relative stance on the principle of dharma. Both are correct in their own stances but, the author Rajaji treats the Dharma of Vibhishana to be the better choice in such a situations and (rightly) absolves Vibhishana of being called a traitor to the Rakhshasa family.<br />Overall, this book is a great read. For people who are well versed with the story of Ramayana, there is nothing new as far as the content is concerned, but the treatment of the epic by Rajaji, who himself was one of the most intellectually distinguished personalities of his time; is commendable.<br />I will rate this book as five. Go pick up your copy or download it on your kindle for just 99 bucks.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16288950702418221865" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_324888955" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/324888955" />
    <a title="Keith Willcock" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8797366-keith-willcock"><img alt="Keith Willcock" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334506662p2/8797366.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/324888955?book_show_action=true">May 06, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Keith Willcock" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Keith Willcock" href="/user/show/8797366-keith-willcock">Keith Willcock</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" 78153549" href="/book/show/13636495-ramayana">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone who likes fun</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer324888955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4348250996655449328">Anyone who wants to know how the gods and goddesses really live should read this. It's a rollicking tale of romance and intrigue that even after 2000 years of telling seems fresh and current. It's sheer humanity defrocks religious dogma that often seems oppressive, overbearing, and out of touch with the real world of human desires and emotions.<br/><br/>A good time is had by all including the bad guys and girls who, having been resoundingly defeated, just seem happy to have resolved some of their karma as</span>
  <span id="freeText4348250996655449328" style="display:none">Anyone who wants to know how the gods and goddesses really live should read this. It's a rollicking tale of romance and intrigue that even after 2000 years of telling seems fresh and current. It's sheer humanity defrocks religious dogma that often seems oppressive, overbearing, and out of touch with the real world of human desires and emotions.<br /><br />A good time is had by all including the bad guys and girls who, having been resoundingly defeated, just seem happy to have resolved some of their karma as they dissolve into nothingness and prepare to return in another incarnation hopefully in a somewhat less combative frame of mind. Each character becomes a lovable reflection of ourselves and even the evil Ravannna shows us a touch of humor and wisdom which may help us to rest a little easier with our own dark side.<br /><br />There is something for ever taste here. Of course the romantics among us are served up a timeless love story, that of Prince Rama and his beautiful and seductive consort Sita, from which modern soap operas could learn a thing or two. Needless to say, love wins out, despite every effort by the forces of evil to separate the devoted duo. <br /><br />Lest the action crowd fear that they will be fed a steady diet of mush mush, be reassured.Your imagination will be stretched to it's limit and there are enough special effects and wizardry to keep Harry Potter fans waving their wands for more. (They can read the Mahabharata next)<br /><br />For those who require that their spirituality be a little less seasoned with sex, and the intrigue and violence that often goes with it do not despair. You have not been forgotten, for in the midst of the worst that humanity can do to itself, as heads are lopped off and blood flows unconditionally, there are doses of profound wisdom which, if swallowed and digested, will definitely keep the devil away. I know. I've tried it and it does work.<br /><br />.....and if none of that appeals to you, read it to your kids. they'll love it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="4348250996655449328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/657949223" />

<div id="review_1517417503" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1517417503" />
    <a title="Satwik" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20236807-satwik"><img alt="Satwik" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454766103p2/20236807.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1517417503?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Satwik" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Satwik" href="/user/show/20236807-satwik">Satwik</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788172763657" href="/book/show/141155.Ramayana">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1517417503" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2752514372007547135">I read this book due to my curiosity as the characters in the book are heard by every Indian on a daily basis. Im no Spiritual guy & i read the book out of curiosity about my favorite Character Hanuman.<br/><br/>firstly one thing the reader finds in the book is the attention to detail given by Valmiki in this epic. And this book is not the original version but a concise version of original Valmiki Ramayana By Rajaji. <br/><br/>Why i Liked this book - <br/> - It gives the list of virtues that a Human being must possess (</span>
  <span id="freeText2752514372007547135" style="display:none">I read this book due to my curiosity as the characters in the book are heard by every Indian on a daily basis. Im no Spiritual guy &amp; i read the book out of curiosity about my favorite Character Hanuman.<br /><br />firstly one thing the reader finds in the book is the attention to detail given by Valmiki in this epic. And this book is not the original version but a concise version of original Valmiki Ramayana By Rajaji. <br /><br />Why i Liked this book - <br /> - It gives the list of virtues that a Human being must possess (these are explicitly described by the author in qualities of Rama (the Protagonist) - Strength, Courage, Purity of heart, Humility, Compassion &amp; forgiveness to others (even towards his enemies), Statesmanship, Wisdom &amp; Adherence to Dharma (dharma doesnt mean laws set by God but Law of the Land) &amp; finally the devotion to his family. The Author describes Rama as Ideal Man)<br /> - It also describes the situations where even the Good guys (Devas like Indra) commit mistakes just to tell us that mistakes are committed by good guys &amp; they could be committed by ignorance (hence cautious one has to remain) &amp; penitence is the way to overcome them<br /> - The author also describes how fate, fear, anger &amp; great sorrowcan corrupt even the noble minds (ex:Kaikeyi, Sita cursing Lakshmana in Forest, Rama saying that its better to destroy this world due to great sorrow of losing Sita) (Great Sorrow indeed imbalances even the great minds) <br />- the author also tells the purpose of an avatar - that God can finish all the business himself but he doesnt do it that way but instead comes in the form of an avatar &amp; suffers all that the avatar is supposed to (sorrowness, hardships etc) so that the Dharma of Humans is est. The main purpose of an avatar is to est the Dharma of Humans <br /><br />What I wasnt satisfied with the book<br />- the discontinuity of certain Characters' appearances in story (probably this is solved in Original Valmiki Ramayana) like Kaikeyi's reaction to the whole event after Bharata is made King &amp; after Rama has gone to Dandaka Forest - how did she react to her husband's death &amp; exile of one of her son &amp; her own son criticizing &amp; disliking her. Some very interesting Characters like Viswamitra etc dont appear again in the story <br />- the reasons to justify the so called controversial acts of Rama (mocking a woman who offered him love (Surpanakha), Killing Vali from behind &amp; asking Sita to enter fire) were also not satisfactory (or probably we should not use our 21st century thinking to describe those old acts) (the act of Rama asking Sita to enter fire is reasonable &amp; logical as the author says just as Parasurama's avatar had ended with his acts, Rama's avatar ended with slaying of Ravana. So the acts post it were the acts of king according to Dharma)<br /><br />Though the book might appear Patriarchal &amp; male centric, we must understand that we cannot compare 2 situations from 2 different space, time dimensions. so we must not judge the situations in the book (where the Sita is shown as Damsel in Distress &amp; Rama comes to help her) from our present understandings<br /><br />This book is not a one time read &amp; must be read several times to understand the inner meaning &amp; to read between the lines. This book entertained me a lot &amp; am sure it will entertain non-religious people.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2752514372007547135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2540614910" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2540614910" />
    <a title="Felix" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22327118-felix"><img alt="Felix" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410543537p2/22327118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2540614910?book_show_action=true">Oct 08, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Felix" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Felix" href="/user/show/22327118-felix">Felix</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9788172763657" href="/book/show/141155.Ramayana">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=sanskrit">sanskrit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=epic">epic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=south-asia">south-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22327118-felix?shelf=classical-period">classical-period</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2540614910" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10647424880304525100">This book is a retelling of Valmiki's ancient poem, the <i>Ramayana</i>. The original poem is tremendously long - usually running for at least three large volumes in translation. For many readers without a scholarly or religious interest in ancient India, this is just too long. For many more (particularly non-Indian) readers, the original may also pose certain cultural challenges.<br/><br/>Rajagopalachari here provides a bridge across these gulfs. His retelling is targeted both at adults and at children. He writ</span>
  <span id="freeText10647424880304525100" style="display:none">This book is a retelling of Valmiki's ancient poem, the <i>Ramayana</i>. The original poem is tremendously long - usually running for at least three large volumes in translation. For many readers without a scholarly or religious interest in ancient India, this is just too long. For many more (particularly non-Indian) readers, the original may also pose certain cultural challenges.<br /><br />Rajagopalachari here provides a bridge across these gulfs. His retelling is targeted both at adults and at children. He writes himself in the epilogue: <br /><br /><i>A word to the children who read these chapters. I have told the story of the Prince of Ayodhya mainly for your sake. Grownup people may read Vaalmeeki and Kamban.</i><br /><br />As such, this isn't the most linguistically rich retelling of the <i>Ramayana</i>. Nevertheless, it's a real joy to read. The story itself follows a great prince, Rama, who through a series of treacherous events ends up living as an exile in the forest. There, he faces various trials, including the kidnapping of his wife, whom he struggles to recover. <br /><br />If that summary sounds familiar, that's probably because it is. The <i>Ramayana</i> falls comfortably within the archetypal tradition of 'hero proving himself in the wilderness' stories. There are similar traits in the <i>Epic of Gilgamesh</i> as well as in the poetry of Ovid, and in no shortage of early English poetry - to name just a few examples. Of course, that is no discredit to the story. This archetypal narrative is one that we hear again and again - and it never gets old.<br /><br />I fear there is a tendency to imagine the literature of ancient times as being something centred around South-Eastern Europe and the Middle East. Besides the <i>Bhagavad Gita</i>, this was my first prolonged exposure to the literary tradition of Ancient India. I had little preconceived notions going into it. I was pleasantly surprised to find a story that reminded of Homer, all the while carrying with it the same weight, and no doubt a similar influence too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10647424880304525100" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2609984282" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2609984282" />
    <a title="Lucas" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53077063-lucas"><img alt="Lucas" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1555813341p2/53077063.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2609984282?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lucas" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lucas" href="/user/show/53077063-lucas">Lucas</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670084180" href="/book/show/8910536-the-ramayana">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=adventures">adventures</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=epic-poetry">epic-poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=sanskrit">sanskrit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=ancient-literature">ancient-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53077063-lucas?shelf=india">india</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2609984282" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7047637510111133253">It never ceases to amaze me the richness of Indian mythology and its world building. At its core, the Ramayana tells a simple eternal story: a princess is kidnapped by evil forces and our hero has to rescue her, destroying evil in the process. It is an archetypical story, yet the Ramayana feels like something completely new, alien even (at least for a Latinamerican reader with little knowledge of Indian literature such as myself) due to the fantastic sense of oral storytelling and complex world.</span>
  <span id="freeText7047637510111133253" style="display:none">It never ceases to amaze me the richness of Indian mythology and its world building. At its core, the Ramayana tells a simple eternal story: a princess is kidnapped by evil forces and our hero has to rescue her, destroying evil in the process. It is an archetypical story, yet the Ramayana feels like something completely new, alien even (at least for a Latinamerican reader with little knowledge of Indian literature such as myself) due to the fantastic sense of oral storytelling and complex world. The orality in this epic is a delight. The richness and creativity in narrative devices is staggering: the author of the Ramayana is a part of the story, there is a 'creation myth' involving the origin of poetry, or the poetic form in which this epic is written, the Ramayana itself is told a number of times in different lengths and styles inside the story. These levels of reality, these meta games, the conflict of god and man, man and evil, make works like this, and its richer, bigger soul brother, The Mahabharata, fresh and unique even today. The Ramayana drags for a long while in the middle sections and I must admit I was bored for long passages, yet one can't help to be moved by this astonishing display of colourful storytelling.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7047637510111133253" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_52068089" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/52068089" />
    <a title="Edward Butler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/238200-edward-butler"><img alt="Edward Butler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442625903p2/238200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/52068089?book_show_action=true">Apr 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Edward Butler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Edward Butler" href="/user/show/238200-edward-butler">Edward Butler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780814731635" href="/book/show/692486.Ram_yana_I">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer52068089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10701599474671050914">The definitive edition of the <i>Ramáyana</i>, unabridged, with the romanized Sanskrit on the facing page and a very useful glossary. This first volume is frequently regarded as having been added on, and indeed lacks the narrative flair that kicks in with the second volume and presumably displays the true artistry of Valmiki. Were the <i>Ramáyana</i> simply a novel, one might say that it would be better to excise or at least severely trim this first book. As it stands, it is somewhat as though tradition had a</span>
  <span id="freeText10701599474671050914" style="display:none">The definitive edition of the <i>Ramáyana</i>, unabridged, with the romanized Sanskrit on the facing page and a very useful glossary. This first volume is frequently regarded as having been added on, and indeed lacks the narrative flair that kicks in with the second volume and presumably displays the true artistry of Valmiki. Were the <i>Ramáyana</i> simply a novel, one might say that it would be better to excise or at least severely trim this first book. As it stands, it is somewhat as though tradition had attached some portion of the <i>Kypria</i> to the beginning of the <i>Iliad</i> to explain how the Trojan war began. From a literary viewpoint, one would prefer the story began as we know it, from the wrath of Achilles; but the prologue would be useful in its own right. This first book of the <i>Ramáyana</i> explains the circumstances of Rama's birth and gets the reader accustomed to the world in which the <i>Ramáyana</i> takes place, in particular the nature of the relationship between Gods and humans and the role of ritual and ascetic practices in that relationship.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10701599474671050914" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/118252.Mahabharata"><img alt="Mahabharata" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1553101923l/118252._SX98_.jpg" />

<script type="text/javascript" charset="utf-8">
//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IsXdpwUzx/vlM4Zi/n9DyWqxfASE4GsCSD1l3sLSJRjR/RMkiJq8z7O1QlCFeNhLNGFh52wbAEiaYolIfj/7iw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('CNJgDs7gV6/K8UoLDyCZeDfodMt+EisAZ+aI5YC2ZOP76q6NQ0ksm5x3jjl0JwL6aThpKJbpQEq1uWRzPFu6cA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

Here, drawing his inspiration from the work of an eleventh-century Tamil poet called Kamban, Narayan has used the talents of a master novelist to recreate the excitement and joy he has found in the original. A work of intricate beauty, it <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11894428975399408684\" style=\"display:none\">The earliest of the four Hindu religious scriptures known as the Vedas, and the first extensive composition to survive in any Indo-European language, the Rig Veda is a collection of over 1000 individual Sanskrit hymns. A work of intricate beauty, it provides a unique insight into early Indian mythology, religion and culture. This selection of 18 of the hymns, chosen for their eloquence and wisdom, focuses on the enduring themes of creation, sacrifice, death, women, the sacred plant soma and the gods. This seems a deliberate souring of an uplifting narrative. Rams refusal to remarry to produce a royal heir adds to the complexity. The intention seems to be to provoke thought on notions of fidelity, property and self-image.\n\nAnd so the mythologist and illustrator Devdutt Pattanaik retells the Ramayana, drawing attention to the many oral, visual and written retellings composed in different times, in different places, by different poets, each one trying to solve the puzzle in its own unique way. \n\nThis book approaches Ram //<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_8020515'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8020515-amar-chitra-katha-mahabharata-vol-1-3?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Amar Chitra Katha Mahabharata Vol. 1-3<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/106888.Anant_Pai?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Anant Pai<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.44 avg rating &mdash; 571 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1972\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer11307686841047600332\">The Mahabharata presents sweeping visions of the cosmos and humanity and intriguing and frightening glimpses of divinity in an ancient narrative that is accessible, interesting, and compelling for anyone willing to learn the basic themes of Hindu cul<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText11307686841047600332\" style=\"display:none\">The Mahabharata presents sweeping visions of the cosmos and humanity and intriguing and frightening glimpses of divinity in an ancient narrative that is accessible, interesting, and compelling for anyone willing to learn the basic themes of Hindu culture. The Mahabharata definitely is one of those creations of human language and spirit that has traveled far beyond the place of its original creation and will eventually take its rightful place on the highest shelf of world literature beside Homer\'s epics, the Greek tragedies, the Bible, Shakespeare, and similarly transcendent works. This collection of 3 Volumes contains 42 different chapters:\n\n1. The Kuru Princes of Hastinapura - Veda Vyasa\'s epic on the Bharata race was first recited in public by his disciple Vaishampayana at the behest of the ageless seer Vyasa himself. The recital took place in the august presence of king Janamejaya, great-grandson of Vyasa, and the many learned sages who had assembled for Janamejaya\'s Sarpa Satra (a twelve-year-long Yajnavv). This volume describes the lineage of the Kuru princes. When Pandu, the father of the Pandavas, dies in the forest, his wife Kunti returns to Hastinapura with the five boys.The great Dronacharya trains the Kuru princes in the art of war. The feats of strength preformed by Bheema, and Arjuna\'s skill at wielding the bow awake jealousy in the minds of Duryodhana and the other Kuru princes.Duryodhana plots the exile of the Pandavas to the city of Varanavata. Acting on the coded warning given by Vidura, the Pandavas manage to escape from the burning house of shellac.\n\nThey live in the guise of mendicants at Ekachakrapura. Later they proceed to Panchala to attend the Swayamvara of Draupadi. Arjuna, the skilful archer, wins Draupadi\'s hand. In order to obey their mother\'s words that they must share everything equally, all the five brothers are married with due ceremony to Draupadi. The Pandavas are recalled to Hastinapura and are given half the Kingdom.\n\n2. The Pandavas in Exile- Volume 1 described the training of the Pandavas and Kauravas, the Pandavas\' escape from the burning house of shellac, their marriage to Draupadi and their subsequent return to Hastinapura.\n\nIn this volume are described the slaying of Jarasandha by Bheema; the subjugation of the other kings by the Pandavas; and the Rajasooya Yajna performed by Yudhishthira. Later, in the game of dice, the Pandavas lose their kingdom and live in exile.At the end of the period of exile, when the Pandavas come back to claim their kingdom, Duryodhana refuses to part with even five villages.\n\n3. On the Battlefield of Kurukshetra- The first two volumes of the Mahabharata described the growing animosity between the Kauravas and the Pandavas, the costly game of dice which led to the banishment of the Pandavas for thirteen years, the events during the exile and the proposal of peace from Dhritarashtra conveyed by Sanjaya to the Pandavas.\n\nThis volume describes the attempts of Krishna to bring about a peaceful settlement between the Kauravas and the Pandavas. They fail and a bitter war is fought for eighteen days. The Pandavas emerge victorious in the end.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"11307686841047600332\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div One keeps you in Swarga; the other raises you <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3054460833408061463\" style=\"display:none\">High above the sky stands Swarga, paradise, abode of the gods. Still above is Vaikuntha, heaven, abode of God.The doorkeepers of Vaikuntha are the twins, Jaya and Vijaya, both whose names mean \'victory\'. One keeps you in Swarga; the other raises you into Vaikuntha.In Vaikuntha there is bliss forever, in Swarga there is pleasure for only as long as you deserve. What is the difference between Jaya and Vijaya? Solve this puzzle and you will solve the mystery of the Mahabharata.In this enthralling retelling of India\'s greatest epic, the Mahabharata originally known as Jaya, Devdutt Pattanaik seamlessly weaves into a single narrative plots from the Sanskrit classic as well as its many folk and regional variants, including the Pandavani of Chhattisgarh, Gondhal of Maharashtra, Terukkuttu of Tamil Nadu and Yakshagana of Karnataka.Richly illustrated with over 250 line drawings by the author, the 108 chapters abound with little-known details such as the names of the hundred Kauravas, the worship of Draupadi as a goddess in Tamil Nadu, the stories of Astika, Madhavi, Jaimini, Aravan and Barbareek, the Mahabharata version of the Shakuntalam and the Ramayana, and the dating of the war based on astronomical data.With clarity and simplicity, the tales in this elegant volume reveal the eternal relevance of the Mahabharata, the complex and disturbing meditation on the human condition that has <span id="freeTextContainer9697342635585265348">Valmiki is celebrated as the poet harbinger in Sanskrit literature. He is the author of the epic Ramayana, based on the attribution in the text of the epic itself.He is revered as the Adi Kavi, which means First Poet, for he discovered the first śloka i.e. first verse, which set the base and defined the form to Sanskrit poetry. The Yoga Vasistha is attributed to him. A religious movement called Va</span>
  <span id="freeText9697342635585265348" style="display:none">Valmiki is celebrated as the poet harbinger in Sanskrit literature. He is the author of the epic Ramayana, based on the attribution in the text of the epic itself.He is revered as the Adi Kavi, which means First Poet, for he discovered the first śloka i.e. first verse, which set the base and defined the form to Sanskrit poetry. The Yoga Vasistha is attributed to him. A religious movement called Valmikism is based on Valmiki's teachings as presented in the Ramayana and the Yoga Vasistha.<br />At least by the 1st century AD, Valmiki's reputation as the father of Sanskrit classical poetry seems to have been legendary. Ashvagosha writes in the Buddhacarita,<br />"The voice of Valmiki uttered poetry which the great seer Chyavana could not compose."<br />This particular verse has been speculated to indicate a familial relationship between Valmiki and Chyavana, as implied by the previous and subsequent verses.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9697342635585265348" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
